                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {

                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="A mosaic monoploid reference sequence for the highly complex genome of sugarcane"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2018 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-07-06"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Sugarcane (Saccharum spp.) is a major crop for sugar and bioenergy production. Its highly polyploid, aneuploid, heterozygous, and interspecific genome poses major challenges for producing a reference sequence. We exploited colinearity with sorghum to produce a BAC-based monoploid genome sequence of sugarcane. A minimum tiling path of 4660 sugarcane BAC that best covers the gene-rich part of the sorghum genome was selected based on whole-genome profiling, sequenced, and assembled in a 382-Mb single tiling path of a high-quality sequence. A total of 25,316 protein-coding gene models are predicted, 17% of which display no colinearity with their sorghum orthologs. We show that the two species, S. officinarum and S. spontaneum, involved in modern cultivars differ by their transposable elements and by a few large chromosomal rearrangements, explaining their distinct genome size and distinct basic chromosome numbers while also suggesting that polyploidization arose in both lineages after their divergence. Sugarcane (Saccharum spp.) is a crop of major economic significance but has complex genome structure. Here, the authors generate a BAC-based monoploid sugarcane reference sequence."/>

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-4060-0292"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-4060-0292</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff13">13</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Queensland" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0000 9320 7537, grid.1003.2, QAAFI (Queensland Alliance for Agriculture and Food Innovation), University of Queensland, St. Lucia, QLD, 4072, Australia" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Jeremy-Schmutz" data-author-popup="auth-Jeremy-Schmutz">Jeremy Schmutz</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff4">4</a>,<a href="#Aff14">14</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="HudsonAlpha Institute for Biotechnology" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0408 3720, grid.417691.c, HudsonAlpha Institute for Biotechnology, Huntsville, AL, 35801, USA" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Joint Genome Institute" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 0449 479X, grid.451309.a, Department of Energy, Joint Genome Institute, Walnut Creek, CA, 94598, USA" /></span></sup> &amp; </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Ang_lique-D_Hont" data-author-popup="auth-Ang_lique-D_Hont" data-corresp-id="c1">Angélique D’Hont<svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-email"></use></svg></a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="UMR AGAP" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2153 9871, grid.8183.2, CIRAD (Centre de Coopération Internationale en Recherche Agronomique pour le Développement), UMR AGAP, F-34398, Montpellier, France" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Montpellier SupAgro" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2172 5332, grid.434209.8, AGAP, Univ Montpellier, CIRAD, INRA, Montpellier SupAgro, 34060, Montpellier, France" /></span></sup> </li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Sugarcane (<i>Saccharum</i> spp.) is a major crop for sugar and bioenergy production. Its highly polyploid, aneuploid, heterozygous, and interspecific genome poses major challenges for producing a reference sequence. We exploited colinearity with sorghum to produce a BAC-based monoploid genome sequence of sugarcane. A minimum tiling path of 4660 sugarcane BAC that best covers the gene-rich part of the sorghum genome was selected based on whole-genome profiling, sequenced, and assembled in a 382-Mb single tiling path of a high-quality sequence. A total of 25,316 protein-coding gene models are predicted, 17% of which display no colinearity with their sorghum orthologs. We show that the two species, <i>S. officinarum</i> and <i>S. spontaneum</i>, involved in modern cultivars differ by their transposable elements and by a few large chromosomal rearrangements, explaining their distinct genome size and distinct basic chromosome numbers while also suggesting that polyploidization arose in both lineages after their divergence.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Sugarcane—a member of the Poaceae family—produces 80% of the world’s sugar and has recently become a primary crop for biofuel production<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="European Commission of Agriculture and rural development. Sugar. &#xA;                    http://ec.europa.eu/agriculture/sugar/index_en.htm&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d41710e858">1</a></sup>. Cultivars are vegetatively propagated, while sugarcane breeding is still essentially focused on conventional methods since sugarcane genetics knowledge has lagged behind that of other major crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Gouy, M., Nibouche, S., Hoarau, J. H., &amp; Costet, L. in Translational Genomics for Crop Breeding: Abiotic Stress, Yield and Quality, Vol. 2, (eds. Varshney, R. &amp; Tuberosa, R.) Ch. 13, 211–237 (John Wiley &amp; Sons, Ames, Chichester and Oxford, 2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d41710e862">2</a></sup>. This is due to the interspecific, polyploid, and aneuploid nature of modern sugarcane cultivar genomes, a complexity that exceeds that of most if not all other crops. Modern sugarcane cultivars are derived from a few interspecific hybridizations performed a century ago between <i>S. officinarum</i> and <i>S. spontaneum</i>, two highly polyploid species. <i>S. officinarum</i> (2<i>n</i> = 8<i>×</i> = 80, <i>x</i> = 10)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="D’Hont, A., Ison, D., Alix, K., Roux, C. &amp; Glaszmann, J. C. Determination of basic chromosome numbers in the genus Saccharum by physical mapping of ribosomal RNA genes. Genome 41, 221–225 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d41710e885">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Screenivasan, T. V., Ahloowalia, B. S. &amp; Heinz, D. J. Sugarcane improvement through breeding. Cytogenetics 5, 211–253 (1987)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d41710e888">4</a></sup> has a high sugar content and is believed to have been domesticated around 8000 years ago from the wild species <i>S</i>. <i>robustum</i> (mainly 2<i>n</i> = 60, 80, and up to 200)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Brandes, E. Origin, dispersal and use in breeding of the Melanesian garden sugarcane and their derivatives, Saccharum officinarum L. Proc. Int. Soc. Sugarcane Technol. 9, 709–750 (1956)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d41710e902">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Grivet, L., Glaszmann, J. C. &amp; D’Hont, A. in Darwin’s Harvest: New Approaches to the Origins, Evolution and Conservation of Crops (ed Motley, T.J.) 49–66 (Columbia University Press, New York, 2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d41710e905">6</a></sup>. <i>S</i>. <i>spontaneum</i> is a wild species with various cytotypes and many aneuploid forms (2<i>n</i> = 5<i>×</i> = 40 to 16<i>×</i> = 128; <i>x</i> = 8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="D’Hont, A., Ison, D., Alix, K., Roux, C. &amp; Glaszmann, J. C. Determination of basic chromosome numbers in the genus Saccharum by physical mapping of ribosomal RNA genes. Genome 41, 221–225 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d41710e928">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Panje, R. &amp; Babu, C. Studies in Saccharum spontaneum. Distribution and geographical association of chromosome numbers. Cytologia 25, 152–172 (1960)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d41710e931">7</a></sup> and was used to incorporate disease resistance, vigor, and adaptability<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Screenivasan, T. V., Ahloowalia, B. S. &amp; Heinz, D. J. Sugarcane improvement through breeding. Cytogenetics 5, 211–253 (1987)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d41710e935">4</a></sup>. Recovery of high sugar production was achieved by backcrossing the first hybrid to <i>S. officinarum</i> and then enhanced via 2<i>n</i> chromosome transmission by the female <i>S. officinarum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bremer, G. A cytological investigation of some species and species-hybrids of the genus Saccharum. Genetica 5, 273–326 (1923)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d41710e948">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bremer, G. Problems in breeding and cytology of sugarcane. Euphytica 10, 59–78 (1961)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d41710e951">9</a></sup>. The resulting cultivars are highly heterozygous, aneuploid, and have 100–130 chromosomes, most of which are derived from <i>S. officinarum</i>, 10–20% from <i>S. spontaneum</i>, and ~10% from interspecific recombinants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="D’Hont, A. et al. Characterisation of the double genome structure of modern sugarcane cultivars (Saccharum spp.) by molecular cytogenetics. Mol. Gen. Genet. 250, 405–413 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d41710e961">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Piperidis, G., Piperidis, N. &amp; D’Hont, A. Molecular cytogenetic investigation of chromosome composition and transmission in sugarcane. Mol. Genet. Genom. 284, 65–73 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d41710e964">11</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#Fig1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-05051-5/MediaObjects/41467_2018_5051_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-05051-5/MediaObjects/41467_2018_5051_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="860" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Schematic representation of the genome of a typical modern sugarcane cultivar. Each bar represents a chromosome, in orange or red when originating from <i>S. officinarum</i> or <i>S. spontaneum</i>, respectively. Chromosomes aligned on the same row are hom(oe)ologous chromosomes (HG). The key characteristics of this genome are the high polyploidy, aneuploidy, bispecific origin of the chromosomes, the existence of structural differences between chromosomes of the two origins, and the presence of interspecific chromosome recombinants</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>In the last 20 years, molecular geneticists have achieved significant advances in developing molecular resources and enhancing the overall understanding of the sugarcane genome. Several geneticmaps have been produced (reviewed in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Zhang, J. et al. in Sugarcane: Physiology, Biochemistry, and Functional Biology (eds. Moore, P. H. &amp; Botha, F. C) Ch. 23, 623–643 (John Wiley &amp; Sons, Ames, Chichester and Oxford, 2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d41710e999">12</a></sup>) mainly based on single-dose markers that are the most informative markers in this high polyploidy context<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wu, K. et al. The detection and estimation of linkage in polyploids using single-dose restriction fragment. Theor. Appl. Genet. 83, 294–300 (1992)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1003">13</a></sup>. None of these maps were saturated, but they revealed that chromosome assortment in modern cultivars results from general polysomy with some cases of preferential pairing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Aitken, K. S., Jackson, P. A. &amp; McIntyre, C. L. A combination of AFLP and SSR markers provides extensive map coverage and identification of homo(eo)logous linkage groups in a sugarcane cultivar. Theor. Appl. Genet. 110, 789–801 (2005)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1007">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Aitken, K. S. et al. A comprehensive genetic map of sugarcane that provides enhanced map coverage and integrates high-throughput Diversity Array Technology (DArT) markers. BMC Genomics 15, 152 (2014)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1007_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="D’Hont, A. et al. A molecular approach to unraveling the genetics of sugarcane, a complex polyploid of the Andropogoneae tribe. Genome 37, 222–230 (1994)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1007_2">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Grivet, L. et al. RFLP mapping in cultivated sugarcane (Saccharum spp.): genome organization in a highly polyploid and aneuploid interspecific hybrid. Genetics 142, 987–1000 (1996)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1007_3">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hoarau, J. Y. et al. Genetic dissection of a modern sugarcane cultivar (Saccharum spp.).I. Genome mapping with AFLP markers. Theor. Appl. Genet. 103, 84–87 (2001)." href="#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1007_4">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jannoo, N., Grivet, L., David, J., D’Hont, A. &amp; Glaszmann, J. C. Differential chromosome pairing affinities at meiosis in polyploid sugarcane revealed by molecular markers. Heredity 93, 460–467 (2004)." href="#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1007_5">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Raboin, L. M. et al. Genetic mapping in sugarcane, a high polyploid, using bi-parental progeny: identification of a gene controlling stalk colour and a new rust resistance gene. Theor. Appl. Genet. 112, 1382–1391 (2006)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1007_6">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Rossi, M. et al. Genomic distribution and characterization of EST-derived resistance gene analogs (RGAs) in sugarcane. Mol. Genet. Genom. 269, 406–419 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1010">21</a></sup>, while the meiosis of modern sugarcane cultivars mainly involves bivalent pairing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bremer, G. A cytological investigation of some species and species-hybrids of the genus Saccharum. Genetica 5, 273–326 (1923)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1014">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Burner, D. M. &amp; Legendre, B. L. Cytogenetic and fertlity characteristics of elite sugarcane clones. Sugar Cane 1, 6–10 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1017">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Price, S. Cytogenetics of modern sugarcanes. Econ. Bot. 17, 97–105 (1963)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1020">23</a></sup>. Meanwhile, comparative mapping with other Poaceae species revealed extensive genome-wide colinearity with sorghum, which thus became a model for sugarcane<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Aitken, K. S. et al. A comprehensive genetic map of sugarcane that provides enhanced map coverage and integrates high-throughput Diversity Array Technology (DArT) markers. BMC Genomics 15, 152 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1024">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Dufour, P. et al. Construction of a composite sorghum genome map and comparison with sugarcane, a related complex polyploid. Theor. Appl. Genet. 94, 409–418 (1997)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1027">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Guimaraes, C. T., Sills, G. R. &amp; Sobral, B. W. Comparative mapping of Andropogoneae: Saccharum L. (sugarcane) and its relation to sorghum and maize. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 94, 14261–14266 (1997)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1027_1">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Le Cunff, L. et al. Diploid/polyploid syntenic shuttle mapping and haplotype-specific chromosome walking toward a rust resistance gene (Bru1) in highly polyploid sugarcane (2n approximately 12x approximately 115). Genetics 180, 649–660 (2008)." href="#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1027_2">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Ming, R. et al. Detailed alignment of saccharum and sorghum chromosomes: comparative organization of closely related diploid and polyploid genomes. Genetics 150, 1663–1682 (1998)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1030">27</a></sup>. QTL studies have been performed to identify genomic regions involved in important agronomic traits, generally revealing minor effects<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Aitken, K. S. et al. Genetic control of yield related stalk traits in sugarcane. Theor. Appl. Genet. 117, 1191–1203 (2008)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1035">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hoarau, J. Y. et al. Genetic dissection of a modern sugarcane cultivar (Saccharum spp.).II. Detection of QTLs for yield components. Theor. Appl. Genet. 105, 1027–1037 (2002)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1035_1">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ming, R., Liu, S. C., Moore, P. H., Irvine, J. E. &amp; Paterson, A. H. QTL analysis in a complex autopolyploid: genetic control of sugar content in sugarcane. Genome Res. 11, 2075–2084 (2001)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1035_2">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Piperidis, N. et al. Comparative genetics in sugarcane enables structured map enhancement and validation of marker-trait associations. Mol. Breed. 21, 233–247 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1038">31</a></sup>, except for the presence of a few major resistance genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Raboin, L. M. et al. Genetic mapping in sugarcane, a high polyploid, using bi-parental progeny: identification of a gene controlling stalk colour and a new rust resistance gene. Theor. Appl. Genet. 112, 1382–1391 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1042">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Al-Janabi, S. M. et al. Identification of a major quantitative trait locus (QTL) for yellow spot (Mycovellosiella koepkei) disease resistance in sugarcane. Mol. Breed. 19, 1–14 (2007)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1045">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Costet, L., Raboin, L. M., Payet, M., D’Hont, A. &amp; Nibouche, S. Mapping of a major QTA for resistance to the yellow leaf disease of sugarcane caused by the Sugarcane yellow leaf virus (Luteoviridae). Plant Breed. 131, 637–640 (2012)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1045_1">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Daugrois, J. H. et al. A putative major gene for rust resistance linked with a RFLP marker in sugarcane cultivar ‘R570’. Theor. Appl. Genet. 92, 1059–1064 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1048">34</a></sup>. New approaches are now being developed, such as genome-wide association (GWA) studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Debibakas, S. et al. Prospecting sugarcane resistance to Sugarcane yellow leaf virus by genome-wide association. Theor. Appl. Genet. 127, 1719–1732 (2014)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1052">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wei, X., Jackson, P. A., McIntyre, C. L., Aitken, K. S. &amp; Croft, B. Associations between DNA markers and resistance to diseases in sugarcane and effects of population substructure. Theor. Appl. Genet. 114, 155–164 (2006)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1052_1">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Gouys, M. et al. Genome wide association mapping of agro-morphological and disease resistance traits in sugarcane. Euphytica 202, 269–284 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1055">37</a></sup> and genomic selection<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Gouy, M. et al. Experimental assessment of the accuracy of genomic selection in sugarcane. Theor. Appl. Genet. 126, 2575–2586 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1059">38</a></sup>, both of which exploit the high level of linkage disequilibrium observed within sugarcane cultivars<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Jannoo, N. et al. Molecular investigation of the genetic base of sugarcane cultivars. Theor. Appl. Genet. 99, 171–184 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1063">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Raboin, L. M., Pauquet, J., Butterfield, M., D’Hont, A. &amp; Glaszmann, J. C. Analysis of genome-wide linkage disequilibrium in the highly polyploid sugarcane. Theor. Appl. Genet. 116, 701–714 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1066">40</a></sup>. Genomic resources such as large expressed sequence tag (EST) libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Vettore, A. L. et al. Analysis and functional annotation of an expressed sequence tag collection for tropical crop sugarcane. Genome Res. 13, 2725–2735 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1070">41</a></sup> and BAC libraries<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Figueira, T. R. et al. A BAC library of the SP80-3280 sugarcane variety (saccharum sp.) and its inferred microsynteny with the sorghum genome. BMC Res. Notes 5, 185 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1075">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tomkins, J. P. et al. A bacterial artificial chromosome library for sugarcane. Theor. Appl. Genet. 99, 419–424 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1078">43</a></sup> have been developed. A sugarcane reference genome would greatly help to achieve further progress on molecular genetics and functional genomics geared toward assisting in the development of improved cultivars. However, sequencing such a highly complex genome poses challenges that have not been addressed in any prior sequencing project due to the high polyploidy, aneuploidy, and interspecific structure of this genome, with a complete set of hom(oe)ologs predicted to range from 10 to 12 copies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Grivet, L. &amp; Arruda, P. Sugarcane genomics: depicting the complex genome of an important tropical crop. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 5, 122–127 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1082">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Souza, G. M. et al. The sugarcane genome challenge: strategies for sequencing a highly complex genome. Trop. Plant Biol. 4, 145–156 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1085">45</a></sup>. This polyploidy results in a total genome size of about 10 Gb for sugarcane cultivars, while the monoploid genome size is about 800–900 Mb, close to that of sorghum (750 Mb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="D’Hont, A. &amp; Glaszmann, J. C. Sugarcane genome analysis with molecular markers, a first decade of research. Proc. Int. Soc. Sugarcane Technol. 24, 556–559 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1089">46</a></sup>.</p><p>In this context, an attractive initial strategy would be to assemble the gene-rich, recombinationally active part (i.e., euchromatin) of a monoploid (i.e., basic) sugarcane genome. Sorghum and rice genome sequencing showed that euchromatin is gene-rich, repeat-poor, and accounts for most of recombination, while being of similar physical size in sorghum and rice (250–300 Mb) despite their overall genome-size difference (450 Mb for rice and 750 Mb for sorghum)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1096">47</a></sup>. Comparative analysis among sugarcane hom(oe)ologous chromosome segments (BAC) showed very high gene colinearity and sequence conservation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Jannoo, N. et al. Orthologous comparison in a gene-rich region among grasses reveals stability in the sugarcane polyploid genome. Plant J. 50, 574–585 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1100">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Garsmeur, O. et al. High homologous gene conservation despite extreme autopolyploid redundancy in sugarcane. New Phytol. 189, 629–642 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1103">49</a></sup>. One haplotype sequence would thus provide a good reference for the other haplotypes (=hom(oe)ologous chromosomes). In addition, these studies revealed a high level of microcolinearity between sorghum and sugarcane<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1107">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jannoo, N. et al. Orthologous comparison in a gene-rich region among grasses reveals stability in the sugarcane polyploid genome. Plant J. 50, 574–585 (2007)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1107_1">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Garsmeur, O. et al. High homologous gene conservation despite extreme autopolyploid redundancy in sugarcane. New Phytol. 189, 629–642 (2011)." href="#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1107_2">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kim, C. et al. Comparative analysis of Miscanthus and Saccharum reveals a shared whole-genome duplication but different evolutionary fates. Plant Cell 26, 2420–2429 (2014)." href="#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1107_3">50</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Wang, J. et al. Microcollinearity between autopolyploid sugarcane and diploid sorghum genomes. BMC Genomics 11, 261 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1110">51</a></sup>. Sorghum could be used as a guide to identify a minimum tiling path (MTP) of BACs covering the euchromatin of a monoploid genome of sugarcane.</p><p>While new sequencing and assembling methods tailored for complex genomes continue to be developed, we deployed this original strategy to produce a first sugarcane reference genome sequence assembly.</p><p>This international initiative focused on the R570 cultivar, which has the best-characterized sugarcane genome to date<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="D’Hont, A. et al. Characterisation of the double genome structure of modern sugarcane cultivars (Saccharum spp.) by molecular cytogenetics. Mol. Gen. Genet. 250, 405–413 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1121">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Grivet, L. et al. RFLP mapping in cultivated sugarcane (Saccharum spp.): genome organization in a highly polyploid and aneuploid interspecific hybrid. Genetics 142, 987–1000 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1124">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hoarau, J. Y. et al. Genetic dissection of a modern sugarcane cultivar (Saccharum spp.).I. Genome mapping with AFLP markers. Theor. Appl. Genet. 103, 84–87 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1127">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Rossi, M. et al. Genomic distribution and characterization of EST-derived resistance gene analogs (RGAs) in sugarcane. Mol. Genet. Genom. 269, 406–419 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1130">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Dufour, P. et al. Construction of a composite sorghum genome map and comparison with sugarcane, a related complex polyploid. Theor. Appl. Genet. 94, 409–418 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1133">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Grivet, L. &amp; Arruda, P. Sugarcane genomics: depicting the complex genome of an important tropical crop. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 5, 122–127 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1136">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Souza, G. M. et al. The sugarcane genome challenge: strategies for sequencing a highly complex genome. Trop. Plant Biol. 4, 145–156 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1140">45</a></sup>. This cultivar was obtained by CERF (now eRcane) in Réunion (<a href="http://www.ercane.re/">http://www.ercane.re/</a>). It has broad adaptability and is used as a parent stock in many breeding stations worldwide, while also being commercially very successful in Réunion, Mauritius, and Guadeloupe. The genome of this cultivar has about 115 chromosomes, including 10% of whole chromosomes derived from <i>S. spontaneum</i> and 10% from <i>S</i>. <i>officinarum/S. spontaneum</i> recombinant chromosomes, with the remaining being whole chromosomes from <i>S. officinarum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="D’Hont, A. et al. Characterisation of the double genome structure of modern sugarcane cultivars (Saccharum spp.) by molecular cytogenetics. Mol. Gen. Genet. 250, 405–413 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1163">10</a></sup>. Genetic maps of this cultivar were constructed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Grivet, L. et al. RFLP mapping in cultivated sugarcane (Saccharum spp.): genome organization in a highly polyploid and aneuploid interspecific hybrid. Genetics 142, 987–1000 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1167">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hoarau, J. Y. et al. Genetic dissection of a modern sugarcane cultivar (Saccharum spp.).I. Genome mapping with AFLP markers. Theor. Appl. Genet. 103, 84–87 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1170">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Rossi, M. et al. Genomic distribution and characterization of EST-derived resistance gene analogs (RGAs) in sugarcane. Mol. Genet. Genom. 269, 406–419 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1173">21</a></sup> (<a href="http://tropgenedb.cirad.fr/tropgene/JSP/index.jsp">http://tropgenedb.cirad.fr/tropgene/JSP/index.jsp</a>) and aligned with sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Dufour, P. et al. Construction of a composite sorghum genome map and comparison with sugarcane, a related complex polyploid. Theor. Appl. Genet. 94, 409–418 (1997)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1184">24</a></sup>. A BAC library of 103,296 clones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Tomkins, J. P. et al. A bacterial artificial chromosome library for sugarcane. Theor. Appl. Genet. 99, 419–424 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1188">43</a></sup> representing 14× monoploid genome coverage and 1.3× total genome coverage of this cultivar<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Grivet, L. &amp; Arruda, P. Sugarcane genomics: depicting the complex genome of an important tropical crop. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 5, 122–127 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1193">44</a></sup> is available and has been used by the research community for comparative structural analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Le Cunff, L. et al. Diploid/polyploid syntenic shuttle mapping and haplotype-specific chromosome walking toward a rust resistance gene (Bru1) in highly polyploid sugarcane (2n approximately 12x approximately 115). Genetics 180, 649–660 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1197">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551–556 (2009)." href="#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1200">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jannoo, N. et al. Orthologous comparison in a gene-rich region among grasses reveals stability in the sugarcane polyploid genome. Plant J. 50, 574–585 (2007)." href="#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1200_1">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Garsmeur, O. et al. High homologous gene conservation despite extreme autopolyploid redundancy in sugarcane. New Phytol. 189, 629–642 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1203">49</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="De Setta, N. et al. Building the sugarcane genome for biotechnology and identifying evolutionary trends. BMC Genomics 15, 540 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1206">52</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Vilela, M. M. et al. Analysis of three sugarcane homo/homeologous regions suggests independent polyploidization events of Saccharum officinarum and Saccharum spontaneum. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 266–278 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1209">53</a></sup>.</p><p>Here, we implemented this strategy using WGP<sup>TM</sup> technology<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="van Oeveren, J. et al. Sequence-based physical mapping of complex genomes by whole genome profiling. Genome Res. 21, 618–625 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d41710e1218">54</a></sup> to align sugarcane BACs from the R570 cultivar with the sorghum genome and identify a MTP of 4660 sugarcane BACs originating from distinct hom(oe)ologous chromosomes that we sequenced, assembled, and annotated. We developed a SNP-based genetic map for the R570 cultivar and performed global genome-wise comparisons between <i>S. officinarum</i>, <i>S. spontaneum</i>, and sorghum. This first sugarcane sequence represents the gene-rich part of the monoploid sugarcane genome.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">A MTP for the gene-rich part of the genome</h3><p>The overall strategy that we used to produce a sugarcane monoploid reference sequence assembly is represented in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#Fig2">2</a>. A total of 20,736 BAC clones from the sugarcane R570 cultivar, representing around two-fold the monoploid genome of sugarcane, were analyzed via WGP. This technology generates Illumina short-read sequences (WGP tags) from BAC clone-restriction fragments. A total of 701,066 WGP tags were obtained and those that were common to several BACs were discarded. Half (222,745) of the remaining WGP tags could be aligned onto the sorghum sequence. Among them, 90,953 WGP tags aligning at a single position allowed anchoring of 11,732 sugarcane BACs onto the ten sorghum chromosomes (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#Tab1">1</a>). Another 577 already-sequenced BAC clones were also anchored onto the sorghum genome. The sugarcane BAC clones were, not surprisingly, mostly distributed in sorghum gene-rich distal chromosomal regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#Fig3">3</a>) as mainly genes are conserved between the two species. Among a total of 12,309 anchored BACs, an MTP of 4660 BAC clones, corresponding to the minimal set of BACs providing the best coverage of the gene-rich part of the 10 sorghum chromosomes, was defined (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Sequencing strategy targeting the sugarcane monoploid genome based on the overall synteny and colinearity conservation within sugarcane hom(oe)ologs and with sorghum. <b>a</b> WGP sequence tags were produced from R570 sugarcane BACs. <b>b</b> WGP sequence tags were aligned onto the sorghum sequence, thus allowing the location of sugarcane BACs on sorghum. <b>c</b> A minimum tiling path of a BAC (MTP) corresponding to a monoploid sugarcane genome was defined and sequenced. <b>d</b> Overlapping BAC sequences were trimmed to construct the single tiling path (STP). <b>e</b> The STP sequence contains BAC contigs that belong to distinct hom(oe)ologous chromosomes. <i>S. officinarum</i> and <i>S. spontaneum</i> chromosome segments are represented in orange and red, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-05051-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink"
